    <script type="text/javascript" nonce="e5b1d5bfc3fe4ecfab7e09a7a5eb039a">

        function getParameter(parameterName) {
            let result = null,
                tmp = [];
            location.search
                .substr(1)
                .split("&")
                .forEach(function (item) {
                    tmp = item.split("=");
                    if (tmp[0] === parameterName) result = tmp[1];
                });
            return result;
        }

        var SeamlessAccessService = {
            spUrl: "/action/ssostart",
            returnURL: window.location.href,
            my_context: 'seamlessaccess.org',
            queryParamsDelimiter: encodeURIComponent('?'),

            getWAYFLessUrl: function (entityId) {
                let redirectURL = getParameter('redirectUri');
                if(redirectURL == null){
                    redirectURL = encodeURIComponent(this.returnURL);
                }
                if(redirectURL.indexOf('saml_referrer') < 0){
                    redirectURL += encodeURIComponent(((redirectURL.indexOf(this.queryParamsDelimiter) !== -1) ? '&' : '?') + 'saml_referrer');
                }
                return this.spUrl + "?idp=" + encodeURIComponent(entityId)
                    + "&redirectUri=" + redirectURL;
            },
            retrieveEntity: function () {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.entities(self.my_context)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // get the results
                            var results = res.data;
                            return resolve(results);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            reject('retrieve entity error: ' + err);
                        });
                });
            },
            updateEntity: function (entitydata) {
                var self = this;
                var ps = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                var jsonObj = {};
                try {
                    //try to parse it, if it is a json string
                    jsonObj = JSON.parse(entitydata.val());
                } catch (e) {
                    jsonObj = entitydata;
                }
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    ps.update(self.my_context, jsonObj)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            // Process the results
                            var ssoInstitutions = res.data;
                            return resolve(ssoInstitutions);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to update the local storage duo to: ' + err);
                            reject(err);
                        });
                })
            },
            removeEntity: function (entityId) {
                var self = this;
                var persistService = new thiss.PersistenceService('https://service.seamlessaccess.org/ps/');

                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    persistService.remove(self.my_context, entityId)
                        .then(function (res) {
                            return resolve(res);
                        }, function (err) {
                            // failed
                            console.log('failed to remove from local storage duo to: ' + err);
                        });
                })
            },
            getAccessibleEntityStatus: function (doi, entityId, entityTitle) {
                return new Promise(function (resolve) {
                    $.ajax({
                        type: 'POST',
                        url: '/action/seamlessAccess',
                        accept: {
                            text: "application/json"
                        },
                        data: {doi: doi, entityid: entityId, entityTitle: entityTitle, eventGroupKey: '5cf86cad-f368-4a7b-a815-07b1760254d4'}

                    }).done(function (status) {
                        return resolve(status);
                    });
                });
            },
            getEntitiesWithAllInfo: function (doi) {
                let self = this,
                    arrayOfPromises = [];
                return new Promise(function (resolve, reject) {
                    self.retrieveEntity().then(function (res) {
                        let allSsoInstitutions = res;
                        for (let i in allSsoInstitutions) {
                            let ssoInstitution = allSsoInstitutions[i];
                            arrayOfPromises.push(new Promise(function (resolve) {
                                self.getAccessibleEntityStatus(doi, ssoInstitution.entity.entityID, ssoInstitution.entity.title)
                                    .then(function (entityStatus) {
                                        resolve({
                                            entityID: ssoInstitution.entity.entityID,
                                            entityTitle: ssoInstitution.entity.title,
                                            entityStatusObj: entityStatus,
                                            entitySsoUrl: self.getWAYFLessUrl(ssoInstitution.entity.entityID)
                                        });
                                    }, function (err) {
                                        console.log('failed to get the entity status: ' + err);
                                    });
                            }));
                        }
                        Promise.all(arrayOfPromises)
                            .then(function (finalResponse) {
                                resolve(finalResponse);
                            });

                    }).catch(function (err) {
                        reject(err);
                    })
                });
            }
        };

        window.addEventListener('load', function () {
            document.querySelectorAll(".sso-institution").forEach(function (institution) {
                var entityId = institution.getAttribute('data-entityid');
                var entityName = institution.getAttribute('data-name');
                var entitydata = {
                    entityID: entityId,
                    title: entityName
                };
                institution.addEventListener("click", function (e) {
                    e.preventDefault();
                    var self = this;
                    SeamlessAccessService.updateEntity(entitydata).then(function (res) {
                        console.log(res);
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    }).catch(function (err){
                        window.location.href = self.href;
                    });
                });
            });
        });

    </script>

   <div class="abstract-group ">
      <section class="article-section article-section__abstract" lang="en" data-lang="en" lang-name="English" id="section-1-en">
         <h2 id="d64245265" class="article-section__header section__title main abstractlang_en main">Abstract</h2>
         <div class="article-section__content en main">
            <p>Sodium-ion batteries (SIBs) draw much attention as alternative charge carrier to lithium because of their abundance and cost-effectiveness. However, SIBs are limited by their low capacity to achieve comparable loadings in graphite. Herein, cyanographene and cyanographite are proposed as materials exhibiting superior storage capacity, facilitating sodium ionization. First-principle calculations are performed to evaluate the sodium adsorption and diffusion of cyanographene and cyanographite. In the calculation, it is shown that sodium atoms diffuse with relatively low activation energy over cyanographene and cyanographite. It is found out that cyanographene and cyanographite have greater sodium-storage capacity than graphite. The high sodium mobility and large sodium-storage capacity demonstrate that cyanographene and cyanographite are a promising anode material for SIBs.</p>
            </div>
      </section>
   </div>
   <div class="pb-dropzone" data-pb-dropzone="below-abstract-group"><!-- Empty dropzone --></div>
   
   <section class="article-section article-section__full">
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adem202301907-sec-0001">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adem202301907-sec-0001-title">1 Introduction</h2>
         
         <p>Lithium ions possess the capability to intercalate within graphite reversibly, achieving significant lithium loadings, extending to a stoichiometry of LiC<sub>6</sub>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adem202301907-bib-0001" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adem202301907-bib-0001_R_d64245257e279" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 1">1</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This process culminates in a specific capacity approximating 372 mAhg<sup>−1</sup>, concomitant with the production of graphite intercalation compounds.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adem202301907-bib-0002" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adem202301907-bib-0002_R_d64245257e286" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 2 - Metall. Mater. Trans. A">2</a>-<a href="#adem202301907-bib-0004" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adem202301907-bib-0004_R_d64245257e286" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 4 - Nat. Chem.">4</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> This reversible, nanoscale phenomenon currently underpins the majority of battery technology. However, sodium, despite its relative abundance and cost-effectiveness as an alternative charge carrier to lithium, is unfortunately limited by its low capacity to achieve comparable loadings in graphite. Its maximum stoichiometry stands at NaC<sub>64</sub>, resulting in a considerably lower capacity of ≈35 mAhg<sup>−1</sup>.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adem202301907-bib-0005" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adem202301907-bib-0005_R_d64245257e296" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 5 - Nature">5</a>-<a href="#adem202301907-bib-0007" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adem202301907-bib-0007_R_d64245257e296" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 7 - Adv. Mater.">7</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It has been reported that hard carbon,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adem202301907-bib-0008" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adem202301907-bib-0008_R_d64245257e302" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 8 - Carbon">8</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/66e5e58c-31a9-4646-be73-372d00c4170a/adem202301907-math-0001.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:14381656:media:adem202301907:adem202301907-math-0001" display="inline" location="graphic/adem202301907-math-0001.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <msub>
                        <mrow>
                           <mtext>Cu</mtext>
                        </mrow>
                        <mn>4</mn>
                     </msub>
                     <msub>
                        <mi mathvariant="normal">S</mi>
                        <mn>4</mn>
                     </msub>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/xtex">$\left(\text{Cu}\right)_{4} \left(\text{S}\right)_{4}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math> nanosheet,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adem202301907-bib-0009" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adem202301907-bib-0009_R_d64245257e321" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 9 - ACS Appl. Energy Mater.">9</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> MXenes<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adem202301907-bib-0010" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adem202301907-bib-0010_R_d64245257e326" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 10 - J. Phys. Chem. C">10</a>, <a href="#adem202301907-bib-0011" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adem202301907-bib-0011_R_d64245257e326" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 11 - Chem. Eng. J.">11</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup><span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/73e6d64d-3bd3-4ab2-952b-59e8793d938f/adem202301907-math-0002.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:14381656:media:adem202301907:adem202301907-math-0002" display="inline" location="graphic/adem202301907-math-0002.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <msub>
                        <mrow>
                           <mtext>CuP</mtext>
                        </mrow>
                        <mn>2</mn>
                     </msub>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/xtex">$\left(\text{CuP}\right)_{2}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>, P2-type <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/a2197ad1-28de-4347-8d0d-56af7fcc3bc1/adem202301907-math-0003.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:14381656:media:adem202301907:adem202301907-math-0003" display="inline" location="graphic/adem202301907-math-0003.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <msub>
                        <mrow>
                           <mtext>Na</mtext>
                        </mrow>
                        <mn>2</mn>
                     </msub>
                     <msub>
                        <mrow>
                           <mtext>Ni</mtext>
                        </mrow>
                        <mn>2</mn>
                     </msub>
                     <msub>
                        <mrow>
                           <mtext>TeO</mtext>
                        </mrow>
                        <mn>6</mn>
                     </msub>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/xtex">$\left(\text{Na}\right)_{2} \left(\text{Ni}\right)_{2} \left(\text{TeO}\right)_{6}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math>,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adem202301907-bib-0012" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adem202301907-bib-0012_R_d64245257e359" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 12 - Mater. Today Energy">12</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Prussian blue,<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adem202301907-bib-0013" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adem202301907-bib-0013_R_d64245257e364" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 13 - InfoMat">13</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> and layered oxide cathodes<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adem202301907-bib-0014" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adem202301907-bib-0014_R_d64245257e370" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 14 - Adv. Energy Mater.">14</a>, <a href="#adem202301907-bib-0015" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adem202301907-bib-0015_R_d64245257e370" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 15 - eScience">15</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> are suggested as potential electrode materials in sodium-ion batteries (SIBs).<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adem202301907-bib-0016" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adem202301907-bib-0016_R_d64245257e375" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 16 - Interdiscipl. Mater.">16</a>, <a href="#adem202301907-bib-0017" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adem202301907-bib-0017_R_d64245257e375" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 17 - Energy Mater. Adv.">17</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>It has been suggested that the weak intercalation of sodium into graphite results from a large ionization energy and ionic radius.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adem202301907-bib-0018" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adem202301907-bib-0018_R_d64245257e382" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 18 - Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA">18</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Descending the periodic table, alkali metals’ ionization energy decreases, while ionic radius increases, leading to weaker electrostatic coupling with graphene. This results in the weakest chemical bond between metal and graphene among alkali metals. Innovative strategies bolstering this electrostatic interaction could foster a new generation of SIBs.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adem202301907-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adem202301907-bib-0019_R_d64245257e387" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Sci. Adv.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Here, cyanographene and cyanographite are proposed as materials exhibiting superior storage capacity, facilitating sodium ionization. Cyanographene is synthesized from fluorographene in 2017, which exhibits low toxicity, high electric conductivity, and good stability.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adem202301907-bib-0020" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adem202301907-bib-0020_R_d64245257e394" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 20 - ACS Nano">20</a>, <a href="#adem202301907-bib-0021" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adem202301907-bib-0021_R_d64245257e394" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 21 - Adv. Sci.">21</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Also, cyanographite, multilayered cyanographene, is synthesized from fluorographite.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adem202301907-bib-0022" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adem202301907-bib-0022_R_d64245257e399" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 22 - J. Phys. Chem. C">22</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> It is considered to be that the electrostatic interaction between cyano groups and sodium can result in a higher capacity than graphite. To demonstrate the application of cyanographene and cyanographite to SIBs, the adsorption and diffusion energy barrier of sodium atoms are investigated in terms of the first-principle calculation.</p>
         </section>
      <section class="article-section__content" id="adem202301907-sec-0002">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adem202301907-sec-0002-title">2 Experimental Section</h2>
         
         <p>The grid-based projector augmented wave software package within density-functional theory calculations was implemented.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adem202301907-bib-0023" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adem202301907-bib-0023_R_d64245257e413" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 23 - Phys. Rev. B">23</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The exchange–correlation of van der Waals vdW–DF<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adem202301907-bib-0024" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adem202301907-bib-0024_R_d64245257e418" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 24 - Phys. Rev. Lett.">24</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> based on Perdew–Burke–Ernzerhof<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adem202301907-bib-0025" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adem202301907-bib-0025_R_d64245257e423" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 25 - Phys. Rev. Lett.">25</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> was implemented with spin polarization to consider the effect of vdW forces. All calculations were performed with a plane-wave cutoff of 400 eV. The 4 × 4 × 1 and 4 × 4 × 4 special k points of the Brillouin zone sampling were applied to cyanographene and cyanographite, respectively.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adem202301907-bib-0026" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adem202301907-bib-0026_R_d64245257e429" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 26 - Phys. Rev. B">26</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The relaxation procedure was executed until the forces on each atom were smaller than 0.02 eV Å<sup>−1</sup>. Bader charge analysis was implemented to understand the charge transfer.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adem202301907-bib-0027" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adem202301907-bib-0027_R_d64245257e436" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 27 - Comput. Mater. Sci.">27</a>, <a href="#adem202301907-bib-0028" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adem202301907-bib-0028_R_d64245257e436" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 28 - J. Phys.: Condens. Matter">28</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> The transition states energies were obtained using the nudged elastic band (NEB) method by considering ten images between each state.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adem202301907-bib-0029" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adem202301907-bib-0029_R_d64245257e442" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 29 - J. Chem. Phys.">29</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup></p>
         
         <p>Here, a sodium atom was placed in cyanographene and cyanographite. Cyanographene was modeled as a supercell (3 × 3) of graphene, with two cyano groups on each side as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adem202301907-fig-0001">1a</a>. In cyanographene, periodic boundary conditions were set in the <i>x</i> and <i>y</i> axes with 12 Å of vacuum in the <i>z</i> axis. Note that the cyanographite was modeled from AA stack. The results of AB stack cyanographite are shown in Supporting Information.</p>

      <section class="article-section__content" id="adem202301907-sec-0003">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adem202301907-sec-0003-title">3 Results and Discussion</h2>
         
         <p>The adsorption of sodium atoms to cyanographene and cyanographite is investigated to understand the possibility of SIBs. In cyanographene, the sodium atom is adsorbed between the cyano group and graphene part as shown in Figure <a href="#adem202301907-fig-0001">1c</a> (CN–Na–G adsorption). The adsorption energy over cyanographene is calculated to be −0.53 eV as shown in <b>Table</b> <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#adem202301907-tbl-0001">1</a>. It is negative and thus the sodium atom is adsorbed in exothermic on cyanographene. The distance between the Na and N of CN is 2.47 Å, while the distance between Na and C of cyanographene is 2.68 Å as shown in Table <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#adem202301907-tbl-0001">1</a>. In contrast, the sodium atom in cyanographite is adsorbed between two CNs in interlayer (CN–Na–NC adsorption) as well as CN–Na–G adsorption, as shown in Figure <a href="#adem202301907-fig-0001">1d,e</a>. The adsorption energy over cyanographite is calculated to be −0.69 eV for CN–Na–G and −1.12 eV for CN–Na–NC as shown in Table <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#adem202301907-tbl-0001">1</a>. They are negative and thus the sodium atom is adsorbed in exothermic on cyanographite. On CN–Na–G adsorption, the distance between Na and N of CN is 2.46 Å, while the distance between Na and C of cyanographite is 2.78 Å. On CN–Na–NC adsorption, the distance between the Na and N of CN is 2.29 Å, while the distance between Na and C is 4.97 Å. Because the sodium atom at CN–Na–NC has interaction with two cyano groups, the adsorption energy for CN–Na–NC of −1.12 eV is larger than CN–Na–G of −0.69 eV as shown in Figure <a href="#adem202301907-fig-0001">1</a>.</p>

         <p>Bader analysis is conducted to determine the charge transfer for the sodium atom and graphene part of cyanographene or cyanographite, as shown in <b>Table</b> <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#adem202301907-tbl-0002">2</a>. Without sodium atoms, the analysis indicates that N of CN is negatively charged by 0.89 electrons for cyanographene and 0.96 electrons for cyanographite. With the sodium atom at CN–Na–G, the analysis indicates that N of CN is negatively charged by 1.14 electrons for cyanographene and cyanographite. Also, it is observed that the sodium atom is positively charged by 0.91 electrons for cyanographene and cyanographite. With the sodium atom at CN–Na–NC, the analysis indicates that N of CN is negatively charged by 1.14 electrons in cyanographite. Also, it is observed that the sodium atom is positively charged by 0.87 electrons in cyanographite. These results indicate an ionic-like interaction between Na and CN in cyanographene and cyanographite. It can be inferred that the interaction fosters sodium atoms adsorption.</p>

         <p>The projected density of state (PDOS) is calculated to understand the electronic structure. PDOS among the graphene part, CN, and Na, is shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adem202301907-fig-0002">2</a>. PDOS among N, C of CN, and Na in cyanographite reveals overlap <i>p</i> state near −7 eV as shown in Figure <a href="#adem202301907-fig-0002">2d,e</a>, while the others have no overlap. The result indicates that there is electron paring between CN and Na.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adem202301907-fig-0002"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0477573b-5c69-4e2d-bc77-bd2c22fa8206/adem202301907-fig-0002-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/0477573b-5c69-4e2d-bc77-bd2c22fa8206/adem202301907-fig-0002-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/8b721abb-aada-44b3-98c2-5d39c0694962/adem202301907-fig-0002-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0477573b-5c69-4e2d-bc77-bd2c22fa8206/adem202301907-fig-0002-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 2<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adem202301907-fig-0002&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadem.202301907" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Projected density of states (PDOS) where the <i>p</i> orbitals of carbon of graphene part, carbon of cyano group, nitrogen of cyano group, and sodium are plotted in gray, black, blue, and red, respectively. a) Cyanographene, b) Cyanographite, c) Cyanographene C-Na-G, d) Cyanographit-eC-NN-aG, and e) Cyanographit-CN-Na.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>The energy barrier for sodium atom diffusion on cyanographene and cyanographite is calculated. Energy profiles are collected in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adem202301907-fig-0003">3</a>. In cyanographene, the diffusion path of the sodium atom is set to connect two CN–Na–G sites as shown in Figure <a href="#adem202301907-fig-0003">3a</a>. The highest energy barrier of sodium atom on cyanographene is calculated to be 0.25 eV for A → B as shown in <b>Table</b> <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#adem202301907-tbl-0003">3</a>. It is relatively lower than 0.48 eV of the energy barrier for lithium atoms on graphite.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adem202301907-bib-0030" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adem202301907-bib-0030_R_d64245257e1130" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 30 - Phys. B: Condens. Matter">30</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> In cyanographite, the diffusion path of sodium atom is set to connect two CN–Na–G sites and two CN–Na–NC sites as shown in Figure <a href="#adem202301907-fig-0003">3b</a>. The highest energy barrier of sodium atom on cyanographite is calculated to be 0.52 eV for B → C as shown in Table <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#adem202301907-tbl-0003">3</a>. An estimation using the Arrhenius expression indicates that an energy barrier ≤0.65 eV will allow facile diffusion at room temperature.<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adem202301907-bib-0019" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adem202301907-bib-0019_R_d64245257e1141" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 19 - Sci. Adv.">19</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup> Thus, sodium atoms smoothly diffuse on cyanographene and cyanographite.</p>

         </div>
         
         <p>PDOS between Na and N in absorption site and transition state indicates the rationale for energy profiles as shown in Figure S5, Supporting Information. In cyanographene energy profile (Figure <a href="#adem202301907-fig-0003">3a</a>), PDOS for A site has no overlap <i>p</i> state between N and Na in the range above 0 eV, while transition state <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/2c2f2b7f-5e9a-49dd-b8e9-17c4ff9b2a09/adem202301907-math-0026.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:14381656:media:adem202301907:adem202301907-math-0026" display="inline" location="graphic/adem202301907-math-0026.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>T</mi>
                        <mrow>
                           <mtext>AB</mtext>
                        </mrow>
                     </msub>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/xtex">$T_{\text{AB}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math> between A and B path has overlap <i>p</i> state near +2 eV as shown in Figure S5a,b, Supporting Information. The overlap <i>p</i> state in <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/6b02f2bf-243c-422f-8191-8a03403f6aad/adem202301907-math-0027.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:14381656:media:adem202301907:adem202301907-math-0027" display="inline" location="graphic/adem202301907-math-0027.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>T</mi>
                        <mrow>
                           <mtext>AB</mtext>
                        </mrow>
                     </msub>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/xtex">$T_{\text{AB}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math> contributes to the energy barrier of 0.25 eV as shown in Table <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#adem202301907-tbl-0003">3</a>. In cyanographite energy profile (Figure <a href="#adem202301907-fig-0003">3b</a>), there is overlap <i>p</i> state between N and Na near −7 eV in both cyanographite A site and transition state <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/8a207447-1572-4724-9e4d-11222266be01/adem202301907-math-0028.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:14381656:media:adem202301907:adem202301907-math-0028" display="inline" location="graphic/adem202301907-math-0028.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>T</mi>
                        <mrow>
                           <mtext>AB</mtext>
                        </mrow>
                     </msub>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/xtex">$T_{\text{AB}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math> as shown in Figure S5c,d, Supporting Information. The result indicates that cyanographite A site and transition state <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/5ef5df41-bcc7-4df9-b0aa-245fb69d9bab/adem202301907-math-0029.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:14381656:media:adem202301907:adem202301907-math-0029" display="inline" location="graphic/adem202301907-math-0029.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>T</mi>
                        <mrow>
                           <mtext>AB</mtext>
                        </mrow>
                     </msub>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/xtex">$T_{\text{AB}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math> have similar bonding states, thus the energy barrier between A and B is lower than 0.01 eV as shown in Table <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#adem202301907-tbl-0003">3</a>. This is because that sodium atom moves around cyano group in A–B path as keeping the distance between N and Na of 2.3 Å. PDOS for cyanographite B site has overlap <i>p</i> state among two nitrogen atoms (top and bottom side) and Na in the range of −8 to −7 eV, while transition state <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/f7c4f963-68b3-49d1-a0f7-552c133dadf8/adem202301907-math-0030.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:14381656:media:adem202301907:adem202301907-math-0030" display="inline" location="graphic/adem202301907-math-0030.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>T</mi>
                        <mrow>
                           <mtext>BC</mtext>
                        </mrow>
                     </msub>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/xtex">$T_{\text{BC}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math> between B and C path has overlap <i>p</i> state only one nitrogen atom (bottom side) and Na in the range of −8 to −7 eV as shown in Figure S5e,f, Supporting Information. The results indicate that the sodium in <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/4567aedb-c248-4535-b55e-c3a450d5a72d/adem202301907-math-0031.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:14381656:media:adem202301907:adem202301907-math-0031" display="inline" location="graphic/adem202301907-math-0031.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>T</mi>
                        <mrow>
                           <mtext>BC</mtext>
                        </mrow>
                     </msub>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/xtex">$T_{\text{BC}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math> loses an interaction from a nitrogen atom, which contributes to the energy barrier of 0.52 eV as shown in Table <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#adem202301907-tbl-0003">3</a>. PDOS for cyanographite C site has overlap <i>p</i> state between N and Na near −7 eV, while transition state <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/c5958775-007d-4a32-87d0-c29ac37d97ec/adem202301907-math-0032.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:14381656:media:adem202301907:adem202301907-math-0032" display="inline" location="graphic/adem202301907-math-0032.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>T</mi>
                        <mrow>
                           <mtext>CD</mtext>
                        </mrow>
                     </msub>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/xtex">$T_{\text{CD}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math> between C and D path has no overlap <i>p</i> state as shown in Figure S5g,h, Supporting Information. The results indicate that sodium in <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/30398001-f6c1-4434-9948-0002006bd65a/adem202301907-math-0033.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:14381656:media:adem202301907:adem202301907-math-0033" display="inline" location="graphic/adem202301907-math-0033.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <msub>
                        <mi>T</mi>
                        <mrow>
                           <mtext>CD</mtext>
                        </mrow>
                     </msub>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/xtex">$T_{\text{CD}}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math> loses an interaction from a nitrogen atom, which contributes to the energy barrier of 0.28 eV as shown in Table <a class="tableLink scrollableLink" title="Link to table" href="#adem202301907-tbl-0003">3</a>.</p>
         
         <p>To evaluate the sodium storage, sodium atom is added to cyanographene and cyanographite one by one until they are saturated by sodium atoms. The distance between cyano group and graphene part for each number of sodium atoms is shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adem202301907-fig-0004">4</a>. In cyanographene, the distance between cyano group and graphene part increases from 1.55 to 4.22 Å when five sodium atoms are added. The results indicate that cyano group is removed from graphene part when five sodium atoms are added to cyanographene as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adem202301907-fig-0005">5a</a>. In cyanographite, the distance between cyano group and graphene part increases from 1.50 to 3.35 Å when six sodium atoms are added. The results indicate that cyano group is removed from graphene part when six sodium atoms are added to cyanographite as shown in Figure <a href="#adem202301907-fig-0005">5b</a>.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adem202301907-fig-0004"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/c24bbb72-f51c-4416-b2ee-9ffe15740ce3/adem202301907-fig-0004-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/c24bbb72-f51c-4416-b2ee-9ffe15740ce3/adem202301907-fig-0004-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/b2cf4a60-71aa-4737-ae4d-ec3c1841ffdf/adem202301907-fig-0004-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/c24bbb72-f51c-4416-b2ee-9ffe15740ce3/adem202301907-fig-0004-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">

         </section>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adem202301907-fig-0005"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/0c1c37ae-82f4-4b21-9a51-6040c349fc6d/adem202301907-fig-0005-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/0c1c37ae-82f4-4b21-9a51-6040c349fc6d/adem202301907-fig-0005-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5c5bd6bb-6bdf-4ad5-ab77-4a76bc70f6a9/adem202301907-fig-0005-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/0c1c37ae-82f4-4b21-9a51-6040c349fc6d/adem202301907-fig-0005-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 5<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adem202301907-fig-0005&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadem.202301907" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">The snapshot of the cyano group divergence. The cyano group is removed from graphene part when a) five sodium atoms for cyanographene and b) six sodium atoms for cyanographite are added.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Moreover, PDOS is calculated to understand the cyano group divergence. PDOS among graphene part, CN, and Na is shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adem202301907-fig-0006">6</a>. In cyanographene with four sodium atoms and cyanographite with five sodium atoms, PDOS between C of CN and graphene part reveals overlap <i>p</i> state in the range of −7 to −6 as shown in Figure <a href="#adem202301907-fig-0006">6a,c</a>. The overlaps indicate electron pairing between graphene part and cyano group. In cyanographene with five sodium atoms and cyanographite with six sodium atoms, PDOS between C of CN and graphene part is not confirmed, while PDOS between N of CN and Na reveals overlap <i>p</i> state in the range of −7 to −4 eV as shown in Figure <a href="#adem202301907-fig-0006">6b,d</a>. Also, there are overlaps <i>p</i> state between C of CN and Na near −5 eV for cyanographene and cyanographite as shown in Figure <a href="#adem202301907-fig-0006">6b,d</a>. The overlaps indicate electron pairing between CN and Na. Therefore, the results are corresponding to cyano group divergence from graphene and forming CN–Na bond as shown in Figure S5,Supporting Information. The process of CN separation has no barrier, thus it might be challenging to reform cyanographene or cyanographite.</p>
         
         <section class="article-section__inline-figure">
            <figure class="figure" id="adem202301907-fig-0006"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/58ed82aa-6bc5-41bc-96eb-c4bb54af08de/adem202301907-fig-0006-m.jpg"><picture>
                     <source srcset="/cms/asset/58ed82aa-6bc5-41bc-96eb-c4bb54af08de/adem202301907-fig-0006-m.jpg" media="(min-width: 1650px)"><img class="figure__image" src="/cms/asset/5d83078e-3a02-441f-b5db-3bc040791e01/adem202301907-fig-0006-m.png" data-lg-src="/cms/asset/58ed82aa-6bc5-41bc-96eb-c4bb54af08de/adem202301907-fig-0006-m.jpg" alt="Details are in the caption following the image" title="Details are in the caption following the image" loading="lazy"></picture></a><figcaption class="figure__caption">
                  <div class="figure__caption__header"><strong class="figure__title">Figure 6<span style="font-weight:normal"></span></strong><div class="figure-extra"><a href="#" class="open-figure-link">Open in figure viewer</a><a href="/action/downloadFigures?id=adem202301907-fig-0006&amp;partId=&amp;doi=10.1002%2Fadem.202301907" class="ppt-figure-link"><i aria-hidden="true" class="icon-Icon_Download"></i><span>PowerPoint</span></a></div>
                  </div>
                  <div class="figure__caption figure__caption-text">Projected density of states (PDOS) where the <i>p</i> orbitals of C of graphene part, C of CN, N of CN, and Na are plotted in gray, black, blue, and red, respectively. a) Cyanographen-4Na, b) Cyanographite-5Na, c) Cyanographene-Na, and d) Cyanographite-6Na.</div>
               </figcaption>
            </figure>
         </section>
         
         <p>Following the conclusion of the last section, sodium atoms are able to be added four for cyanographene and five for cyanographite as shown in <b>Figure</b> <a href="#adem202301907-fig-0007">7</a>. The closest distance between two sodium atoms is 3.58 Å for cyanographene and 3.59 Å for cyanographite. The two values of the closest distance between sodium atoms are larger than the bond length of the sodium dimer (experimental bond length is 3.08 Å<sup>[</sup><span><sup><a href="#adem202301907-bib-0031" class="bibLink tab-link" id="adem202301907-bib-0031_R_d64245257e1587" data-tab="pane-pcw-references" aria-label="Reference 31 - Phys. Rev. B">31</a></sup></span><sup>]</sup>). Therefore, sodium atoms might not agglutinate each other in cyanographene and cyanographite. The number of alkali metals per 100 graphene carbons is calculated to be 22.2 for cyanographene <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/b1c486fa-bfbf-4f8d-9fb0-df1252750a37/adem202301907-math-0034.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:14381656:media:adem202301907:adem202301907-math-0034" display="inline" location="graphic/adem202301907-math-0034.png">
               <semantics>
                  <mrow>
                     <msub>
                        <mrow>
                           <mtext>Na</mtext>
                        </mrow>
                        <mn>4</mn>
                     </msub>
                     <msub>
                        <mi mathvariant="normal">C</mi>
                        <mrow>

                        </mrow>
                     </msub>
                     <mo>−</mo>
                     <mtext>CN</mtext>
                  </mrow>
                  <annotation encoding="application/xtex">$\left(\text{Na}\right)_{4} \left(\text{C}\right)_{18} - \text{CN}$</annotation>
               </semantics></math> and 27.8 for cyanographite <span class="fallback__mathEquation" data-altimg="/cms/asset/82d20eb6-0ffc-4406-86c2-3f4ec6c42662/adem202301907-math-0035.png"></span><math altimg="urn:x-wiley:14381656:media:adem202301907:adem202301907-math-0035" display="inline" location="graphic/adem202301907-math-0035.png">

                  <mrow>
                     <msub>

                     </msub>
                  </mrow>

               </semantics></math> are 4 and 18 for cyanographene, 5 and 18 for cyanographite, 1 and 6 for lithium-graphite, and 1 and 64 for sodium–graphite, respectively. The formula for cyanographene and cyanographite is derived from calculated model as shown in Figure <a href="#adem202301907-fig-0007">7</a>. This result indicates that cyanographene and cyanographite have greater metal storage capacity than graphite. Hence, cyanographene and cyanographite can be used as an anode material for SIBs with a very high capacity.</p>

            <figure class="figure" id="adem202301907-fig-0007"><a target="_blank" href="/cms/asset/48fa0a9a-9e7a-49a3-9802-c58ddc90aae3/adem202301907-fig-0007-m.jpg"><picture>

      <section class="article-section__content" id="adem202301907-sec-0004">
         
         <h2 class="article-section__title section__title section1" id="adem202301907-sec-0004-title">4 Conclusion</h2>
         
         <p>Density-functional theory calculations are conducted to elucidate the potential utility of cyanographene and cyanographite in SIBs. Observations reveal that the sodium atom in cyanographene is adsorbed between cyano group and graphene part while sodium atom in cyanographite is adsorbed between two cyano groups as well as between cyano group and graphene part. Bader charge analysis delineates the ionic-like interaction between the sodium atom and cyano group, promoting sodium atom adsorption. The PDOS suggests electron coupling between the sodium atom and the cyano group in cyanographite, contributing to adsorption energy of −1.12 eV for the sodium atom at the CN–Na–NC. The highest energy barrier for sodium atom diffusion on cyanographene and cyanographite is calculated to be 0.25 and 0.52 eV, respectively. Consequently, sodium atoms smoothly diffuse on cyanographene and cyanographite. Upon the addition of five sodium atoms to cyanographene and six to cyanographite, the cyano group detaches from the graphene part, subsequently interacting with the sodium atoms. The number of alkali metals per 100 graphene carbons is calculated to be 22.2 for cyanographene, 27.8 for AA stack cyanographite, and 16.7 for AB stack cyanographite, which is higher than 1.6 for sodium graphite. Thus, cyanographene and cyanographite have greater sodium storage capacity than graphite. Due to interatomic spacings, sodium atoms might not coalesce within cyanographene and cyanographite, as the minimum distance between adjacent sodium atoms exceeds the bond length of the sodium dimer. Given the elevated sodium mobility and significant sodium-storage capacity, it is evident that both cyanographene and cyanographite offer promising prospects as anode materials for SIBs.</p>
         </section>
      <div class="article-section__content">
